601 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Collins v. American Buslines


No. 5977


Supreme Court of Arizona


79 Ariz. 220; 286 P.2d 214; 1955 Ariz. LEXIS 153



June 28, 1955 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Award affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner widow, individually and on behalf of her child, sought review by certiorari of a determination of the Arizona Industrial Commission that held that it was without jurisdiction to hear a claim for compensation for the death of petitioner's husband, who was allegedly killed while in the course of his employment.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner's husband was killed in an accident that occurred when he was driving a bus route between states from a home terminal in another state where he was paid and lived with his family. Petitioner sought worker's compensation, and the commission found that the deceased was covered by the workmen's compensation provisions of the other state and concluded that it was without jurisdiction to entertain petitioner's claim because the decedent was not regularly employed in the state. Petitioner appealed from the determination, and the court affirmed the decision. The court held that the decedent was employed within the meaning of the statute but that application of the state's workmen's compensation laws would constitute an undue burden on interstate commerce. The court declined jurisdiction on the basis that to require worker's compensation coverage in situations where the deceased was covered by another state's system would impose an undue burden on interstate commerce by requiring employers to pay multiple premiums in states in which they had employees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the determination of the commission that it was without jurisdiction to hear the petitioner's claim for worker's compensation benefits following the death of her husband.

 

602 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Farnsworth v. Hubbard


No. 5718


Supreme Court of Arizona


78 Ariz. 160; 277 P.2d 252; 1954 Ariz. LEXIS 147



November 29, 1954 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied January 18, 1955.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, foreign administrator, domiciliary administratrix, and heirs, sought review of a judgment from the trial court (Arizona), which granted a permanent injunction, specific performance, and damages to appellee claimant who had filed suit to enforce a contract to purchase the decedent's lead mine in Mexico.


OVERVIEW: The claimant had taken possession of a lead mine in Mexico pursuant to a contract to purchase the mine from the decedent. A Mexican court appointed a foreign administrator and had authorized the administrator to take possession of the mine. The claimant filed suit in Arizona to enforce the contract. The trial court granted the claimant specific performance, awarded damages for his dispossession of the mine, and permanently enjoined the foreign administrator and all heirs from interfering with the claimant's possession. On appeal, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court reversed all aspects of the judgment and the injunction which affected the foreign administrator in his individual and representative capacity and held that the trial court erred in assuming personal jurisdiction over the foreign administrator. The court affirmed the judgment and injunction against the other heirs and administrator. The court held that the trial court did not err in granting a default judgment for specific performance of the contract as a sanction for the heirs' willful refusal to comply with discovery orders because the claimant was prejudiced by the delay.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and injunction against the foreign administrator in his individual and representative capacity and ordered a new trial on the order to show cause against him in his individual capacity. The court affirmed the judgment for specific performance and damages and the injunction against the heirs and the domiciliary administrator.

 

603 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Childress


No. 1054


Supreme Court of Arizona


78 Ariz. 1; 274 P.2d 333; 1954 Ariz. LEXIS 123; 46 A.L.R.2d 1169



September 27, 1954 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction by the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona) following a jury trial for driving a vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor in violation of 1950 Ariz. Sess. Laws 1st Spec. Sess. ch. 3, § 54, appearing as § 66-156 (Cum. Supp. 1952). The trial court denied defendant's motion for dismissal. Defendant alleged error in the jury instructions, which paraphrased § 66-156.


OVERVIEW: A highway patrolman stopped defendant's car, which was weaving over the road at an excessive speed. The patrolman testified that defendant exhibited signs of alcohol intoxication and that he admitted to drinking. Defendant gave written permission for a blood-alcohol test, which showed a level of 0.20 percent. Defendant objected to the trial court's jury instructions, which included portions of § 66-156, and instructed the jury that upon proof of a certain alcohol content in the blood, defendant was presumed to have been under the influence. On appeal, the court affirmed. The jury instructions correctly instructed the jury on the elements of the offense and the State's burden of proof to show beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant was driving under the influence. The trial judge's reading of the statute in the jury instructions was exclusively a statement of law and did not constitute his opinion on the evidence contrary to Ariz. Const. art. 6, § 12. The presumption of intoxication based on blood-alcohol content under § 66-156 had a rational connection to facts established by medical science, and did not change the burden of proof or violate defendant's due process rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for driving a vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor.

 

604 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Tucson v. Garrett


No. 5874


Supreme Court of Arizona


77 Ariz. 73; 267 P.2d 717; 1954 Ariz. LEXIS 176



February 23, 1954 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied May 18, 1954.



DISPOSITION: Alternative writ of prohibition made permanent.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city asked the court for a writ of prohibition to restrain respondent superior court judges from enforcing a temporary restraining order enjoining and restraining the city from considering petitions for annexing property or from passing any resolution or ordinance with the purpose of annexing any territory on the basis of the petitions.


OVERVIEW: Based on a complaint by some property owners, the superior court issued a temporary restraining order enjoining and restraining the city from considering petitions for annexing property or from passing any resolution or ordinance with the purpose of annexing any territory on the basis of the petitions. The city asked the court for a writ of prohibition to restrain the superior court judges from enforcing the temporary restraining order. The superior court judges contended that Ariz. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 26-104 (1939), which conferred upon municipalities in Arizona the power to annex territory, contained an implied condition that the petition itself describe or delineate the territory to be annexed. The court made the writ of prohibition permanent. The court held that: (1) if the court read into the statute by implication the requirement that the petitions had to define the area, the court would thereby, to a substantial degree, dissipate the city's discretionary powers expressly given; and (2) such a construction would leave the city commission with only two choices, either to permit applicants for annexation to define the city's boundaries or to forego the right of expansion.


OUTCOME: The court made permanent the writ of prohibition restraining the superior court judges from enforcing the temporary restraining order against the city.

 

605 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Jones v. Lockhart


No. 5857


Supreme Court of Arizona


76 Ariz. 390; 265 P.2d 447; 1953 Ariz. LEXIS 179



December 26, 1953 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment for plaintiff ousting defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff State of Arizona, upon the relation of the Attorney General, brought an original action in quo warranto asking that the court adjudge defendant appointee as not entitled to hold or enjoy the office of state senator from Apache County, Arizona. The State also sought, pursuant to Ariz. Code Ann. § 28-301 (1939), a judgment of ouster against the appointee, who had been appointed by the governor for a short term, effective November 2, 1953.


OVERVIEW: Prior to a proposed 1953 amendment to Ariz. Const. art. IV, pt. 2, § 1(1), the State Senate had been composed of 19 members, with one being from Apache County. The amendment stated that the Senate shall consist of two members from each county. When it appeared that the amendment had been carried by a majority at special election, the governor issued a proclamation declaring it to be law. He apparently considered that there was a newly-created additional office and that he was empowered to fill the vacancy. The court found that when the appointee took an oath of office pursuant to a purported appointment by the governor, entered Senate chambers, and claimed to be a duly qualified senator, he became more than a mere volunteer or interloper. He thus brought himself within the terms of Ariz. Code Ann. § 28-301 (1939) as one who "usurps, intrudes into or who unlawfully holds or exercises any public office." The court was convinced that the appointee purported to hold a nonexistent term of office. Finding that the amendment did not create a "short term" and that the new term set forth in the amendment had not yet come into existence, the court held that there was no vacancy to be filled.


OUTCOME: The court entered judgment for the State. The appointee was ordered ousted and excluded from the State Senate.

 

606 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Rubenstein Const. Co. v. Salt River Project Agr. Improvement & Power Dist.


No. 5790


Supreme Court of Arizona


76 Ariz. 402; 265 P.2d 455; 1953 Ariz. LEXIS 181



December 21, 1953 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant construction company sued appellee power district, seeking the return of monies paid by the company to the district for utility connection charges for dwelling units built by the company. A trial court, Arizona, granted the district's motion for summary judgment. The company appealed.


OVERVIEW: The company constructed 500 dwelling units at an air force base. Each unit was served by the district, with electrical energy for lighting, etc. As a condition precedent to such service, the district demanded from the company a connection charge of $ 25 per unit, which was paid. The company claimed that the service charge was prohibited by Az. Code Ann. § 76-109 (1939), which stated that any public service corporation demanding and receiving a deposit of money from any consumer shall have paid to such consumer eight percent interest per annum thereon. The district claimed that it was not subject to § 76-109 because it was a political subdivision and municipal corporation. The court held that the district was either at all times a political subdivision of the state, or it never was. In the case at bar, the district, which was incorporated within the Constitution of Arizona in 1940, was not a public service corporation. Consequently, § 76-109 had no application to the facts of the case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

607 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Hudson v. Kelly


No. 5817


Supreme Court of Arizona


76 Ariz. 255; 263 P.2d 362; 1953 Ariz. LEXIS 167



November 13, 1953 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Order directing issuance of peremptory writ of mandamus.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In petitioner company's action against respondent State Treasurer of the State of Arizona seeking a peremptory writ of mandamus ordering the state treasurer to pay a warrant issued by the state auditor, the court addressed the reasons why it ordered the writ to issue and why it found that the Financial Administration Act of 1953 was unconstitutional by violating Ariz. Const. art. V, § 1.


OVERVIEW: The company sought a writ to compel the state treasurer to pay a warrant issued by the state auditor to the company in payment of tires. After the act's enactment, state purchases had to be made through the state purchasing agent, as did all supply contracts made by the commissioner of finance appointed under the act. The act also declared void all contracts otherwise made. Section 10(a) of the Act stated that the auditor was to be under the supervision of the commissioner of finance. The court confirmed the order that directed the issuance of the writ. The Act violated the Arizona Constitution because it attempted to destroy the constitutional office of auditor and frustrated the purpose of the office. A constitutional officer's powers could not be removed indirectly or directly. Ariz. Const. art. IX, § 5, precluded any state expenditure except as provided by law, and Ariz. Const. art. V, § 1, provided that state money could only be spent on claims audited by the constitutional auditor. The Act's severability clause was useless because the valid and invalid parts of the Act were inextricably entwined and interdependent in subject matter, purpose, and meaning.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed its previous order that directed the issuance of the peremptory writ of mandamus ordering the treasurer to pay the warrant issued by the state auditor.

 

608 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Tucson, v. Polar Water Co.


No. 5607


Supreme Court of Arizona


76 Ariz. 126; 259 P.2d 561; 1953 Ariz. LEXIS 145



July 13, 1953 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed a decision of the trial court (Arizona), which entered judgment for plaintiff water company in its action to enjoin the city from extending its water service into an addition which had been recently annexed to the city, and to enjoin the city from servicing customers of the water company.


OVERVIEW: The water company sought to enjoin the city from extending city water service into a recently annexed addition and from serving the water company's customers. The trial court entered judgment for the water company, and the city appealed. The court held that: (1) Ariz. Code Ann. § 16-604 (1939) was not applicable because the water company was not operating as a public utility under a franchise granted by the city, the city as a municipal corporation did not receive any water service from the water company, and § 16-604 was inoperative as against a home rule charter; (2) the certificate of convenience and necessity issued to the water company by the Corporation Commission did not amount to an exclusive franchise; (3) the city had the right, under a constitutional grant carried into execution by the charter of the city, to engage in furnishing water to residents both within and without the corporate limits of the city, the city could do so without purchasing the property of the water company, and any damage suffered by the water company as a result thereof was damnum absque injuria. The judgment was reversed with directions to dismiss the water company's complaint.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment enjoining the city from extending its water service into an annexed addition and from servicing the water company's customers with directions to dismiss the water company's complaint.

 

609 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State Bd. of Barber Examiners v. Edwards


No. 5629


Supreme Court of Arizona


76 Ariz. 27; 258 P.2d 418; 1953 Ariz. LEXIS 123



June 15, 1953 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied July 7, 1953.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the State Board of Barber Examiners, sought review of a judgment entered in the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona) granting appellee barber's motion for judgment on the pleadings and enjoining the Board from enforcing price provisions in a statute the court had found unconstitutional on a prior appeal. The Board sought a re-examination of the question of the constitutionality of the statutes affected by the prior opinion.


OVERVIEW: The barber alleged that the enforcement of a statute that related to price fixing was unconstitutional. The court found no rational relationship between the price fixing and the preservation of the public health, safety, and general welfare. The cause was remanded to the trial court wherein the motion for judgment on the pleadings was granted. The Board claimed that the court was without jurisdiction to entertain the former appeal because no appealable order had been entered and that the former opinion disposed of the constitutional question without any facts from which it could be determined whether the price fixing provisions were reasonably related to the public health, safety, and welfare. The court affirmed. The questioned order was a direct order of dismissal, which was appealable. The constitutionality of a statute could be determined by considering its language and the material facts of which the court could take judicial notice. It was not the province of the court to examine evidence for the purpose of deciding a question, which the legislature had already decided. It's function was to determine whether the legislative decision was without rational basis.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

610 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Ferguson v. Superior Court


No. 5767


Supreme Court of Arizona


76 Ariz. 31; 258 P.2d 421; 1953 Ariz. LEXIS 124



June 8, 1953 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Writ of prohibition, as amended, made permanent.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, the Superior Court of Maricopa County and a judge, sought to quash a writ of prohibition requiring respondents to refrain from action in a suit involving the appointment of a conservator for a company. Petitioners, judgment creditors and a creditor of the company, sought the writ alleging that Ariz. Code Ann. § 53-1416(3) (Cum. Supp. 1939), which gave the superior court authority to appoint the conservator, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners alleged that the appointed conservator was arbitrarily paying some creditors and denying claims of other creditors of the company. Petitioners sought to have the property of the company released from the superior court's jurisdiction. The court amended the writ and ordered it to be permanent. The court held as follows: (1) assuming the unconstitutionality of § 53-1416(3) before it was amended, the superior court had no power to appoint a conservator and permit him to take possession of the company's properties; (2) the subsequent amendment to § 53-1416(3) did not have the effect of validating the original appointment for the reason that it was only remedial in character; (3) the superior court had, without jurisdiction, appointed the so-called conservator and turned over to him certain properties of the company; and (4) common justice demanded that so far as possible the property was to be restored to its legal position prior to the appointment.


OUTCOME: The court amended the writ to restrain respondents from taking any further proceedings in the action except such as was necessary to preserve the properties in its custody, to discover those entitled thereto, and to make restitution so far as legally possible. The court ordered the writ be made permanent as so amended.

 

611 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Gillespie Land & Irrigation Co. v. Buckeye Irr. Co.


No. 5273


Supreme Court of Arizona


75 Ariz. 377; 257 P.2d 393; 1953 Ariz. LEXIS 231



May 18, 1953 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervenor land and irrigation company appealed an order from a trial court (Arizona), which dismissed the company's complaint in intervention, which complaint in intervention had theretofore been filed in an action between plaintiffs, canal and water companies, and defendant water users' association. The association was but one of over 5000 defendants.


OVERVIEW: The purpose of the original litigation was to determine the rights of the canal company, water company, and users' association in and to the waters of a river system. The company, by its complaint in intervention, alleged that it had certain rights in to the river. Parties on both sides sought to dismiss the complaint in intervention and the trial court granted the motion. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the trial court was sitting for the very purpose of determining the extent, priorities, and status of the company's claimed rights. When no proof was offered on the extent of appropriation, that is, the quantity of water beneficially used and the particular lands upon which used, the motion to dismiss was presented and there was nothing for the trial court to do other than dismiss the complaint. No evidence having been offered as to the extent of the company's claimed appropriation, the trial court was not in a position to determine whether the company had made an appropriation nor could it determine its priority or status even if it were assumed that the appropriation could have been or was made under any of the suggested or claimed methods of appropriation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment dismissing the irrigation company's complaint in intervention.

 

612 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Schrey v. Allison Steel Mfg. Co.


No. 5751


Supreme Court of Arizona


75 Ariz. 282; 255 P.2d 604; 1953 Ariz. LEXIS 213



April 6, 1953 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged the decision from the trial court (Arizona), which granted appellee in-state contractor a writ of mandamus to compel the city to award it a contract to construct a water tank after the contractor had claimed a preference but the contract had been awarded to another company.


OVERVIEW: The city opened bids for a contract to construct a water tank. An out-of-state contractor had the lowest bid and was awarded the contract, but the in-state contractor filed for a writ of mandamus to compel the city to grant it the contract instead based on the preference it claimed under Ariz. Code Ann. § 56-107 (1939). The writ was granted and the city appealed, arguing that § 56-107 was unconstitutional in light of Ariz. Code Ann. § 10-610 (1939). The court upheld as constitutional certain criteria listed in § 10-610 in view of the principle that the right to contract was a qualified right where the public interest was involved and could be restricted if there was a reasonable basis for the restriction. The court held that the state, in contracting for the expenditure of tax money, had a reasonable basis for granting a five percent preference to contractors who, through the payment of taxes for two years, had made a contribution to the funds from which they were to reap a benefit. The court upheld the grant of the writ of mandamus, finding the in-state contractor to be the lowest responsible bidder.


OUTCOME: The judgment granting the writ of mandamus compelling the city to award the contract to construct a water tank to the in-state contractor was affirmed.

 

613 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Gastelum


No. 1031


Supreme Court of Arizona


75 Ariz. 271; 255 P.2d 203; 1953 Ariz. LEXIS 211



March 30, 1953 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment reversed and cause remanded with directions to grant new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Pima County (Arizona), which entered a jury verdict convicting defendant of wilfully and unlawfully possessing marijuana in violation of the Arizona Uniform Narcotics Act of 1935, Ariz. Code Ann. § 68-801 et seq. (1939). Defendant alleged, inter alia, that his right to a fair trial had been violated by the misconduct of the prosecutor.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possession and control of marijuana in violation of the Arizona Uniform Narcotics Act of 1935 (Act), Ariz. Code Ann. § 68-801 et seq. (1939). On appeal, defendant alleged that the title of the Act was insufficient to inform defendant as to whether it embraced the imposition of penalties for its violation. Defendant claimed that the title was an unconstitutional violation of Ariz. Const. art. 4, part 2, § 13. The court disagreed, holding that the general knowledge of the evils of narcotic drugs would have suggested to the ordinarily reasonable and prudent person that the legislation would be regulative and restrictive in character and would provide punishments for its violation. The court did hold that the prosecutor violated Ariz. Code Ann. § 44-1004 (1939) by repeatedly attempting to elicit on cross-examination admissions from defendant indicating a former conviction. Because the prosecutor's misconduct deprived defendant of a fair trial, the court remanded for a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded for a new trial.

 

614 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Arizona State Tax Comm'n v. Ensign


No. 5609


Supreme Court of Arizona


75 Ariz. 220; 254 P.2d 1029; 1953 Ariz. LEXIS 205



March 24, 1953 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied May 14, 1953. See 257 P.2d 392.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Arizona State Tax Commission sought review from a judgment of the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona), which found in favor of appellee taxpayers in an action by the taxpayers to recover taxes illegally collected.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers were engaged in the business of selling, installing and servicing deep well turbine pumps in Arizona. The Commission made a tax assessment based on the taxpayers' unreported sales as disclosed by an audit. The taxpayers argued that their sales of merchandise as a licensee under Ariz. Code Ann. § 73-1301 et seq., (1939), were not to be included in the gross proceeds of sales, where such sales had been consummated by delivery freight prepaid. The court reversed, concluding that (1) the tax in Ariz. Code Ann. § 73-1303 was upon the privilege of engaging in business measured by the amount or value of business done within the State of whatever character; (2) a state could, without unduly burdening interstate commerce, tax all the revenue or income that was the result of a business' activities within the State; and (3) the taxpayers were not a branch office of the manufacturer, but were an independent domestic business and the gross proceeds of their sales were the result of their business activities within the State.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment with directions to deny the taxpayers any recovery in an action to recover taxes illegally collected by the Arizona State Tax Commission.

 

615 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Marana Plantations


No. 1033


Supreme Court of Arizona


75 Ariz. 111; 252 P.2d 87; 1953 Ariz. LEXIS 182



January 15, 1953 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed an order of the Pima County trial court (Arizona), which dismissed a direct information that charged defendant with violating regulations adopted by the state board of health pursuant to 1941 Ariz. Sess. Laws ch. 105. The trial court held that 1941 Ariz. Sess. Laws ch. 105, § 6, which enabled the board to pass the regulations was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant operated an agricultural labor camp and the information charged defendant with the violation of several sanitation regulations. The trial court held that § 6 unconstitutionally delegated legislative power to an administrative board and that the regulations were unconstitutional in that they were discriminatory and were of a local or special nature. On appeal, the court affirmed. Section 5 made it the duty of the board to formulate general policies affecting the public health. The formulation of policies was for the legislature and administrative rules had to be within the framework of policies that the legislature had sufficiently defined. Section 6 supra gave the board power to regulate sanitation and sanitary practices in the interests of public health and to protect and promote the public health and prevent disability and mortality. Such power was too broad and allowed the board to wander with no guide or criterion. The board could not have unrestrained power to regulate sanitation. The objectionable features mentioned were severable and did not poison the balance of the Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the direct information that charged defendant with violating the board's sanitation regulations.

 

616 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Industrial Comm'n v. Pressley


No. 5634


Supreme Court of Arizona


74 Ariz. 412; 250 P.2d 992; 1952 Ariz. LEXIS 225



November 24, 1952 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Writ of mandamus made peremptory.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state industrial commission (commission) filed a petition for a writ of mandamus which challenged the trial court's (Arizona) denial of the commission's motion to intervene in an injured party's action to recover damages for his injuries from a third-party.


OVERVIEW: The injured employee was injured by a gas explosion while working. The injured employee accepted accident benefits from the commission, but returned its check for compensation. Subsequently, the injured party filed a tort action against a third party to recover damages for his injuries. The commission filed a motion to intervene in the suit on the grounds that the injured party had no right to sue because he had elected compensation from the commission and the commission was subrogated to the injured party's rights against the third party. The trial court denied the commission's motion and the commission filed a writ of mandamus. The court made the writ of mandamus peremptory. The court held that: (1) the trial judge, as a matter of law, should determine whether the injured employee made an election of remedies; (2) the commission had the right of subrogation of the injured employee's right against the third party if it was determined that the injured employee had elected to take compensation; (3) the commission had the right of subrogation for accident benefits the injured employee received; and (4) the commission had the right to intervene in the action.


OUTCOME: The court made the writ of mandamus peremptory and ordered that the commission be allowed to intervene; that the commission be allowed subrogation to any and all of injured employee's rights against the third party for payments made for accident benefits received by the injured employee; and that the trial judge hear and decide the question of election as a matter of law before proceeding with the trial of the case on its merits.

 

617 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Adams v. Bolin


No. 5680


Supreme Court of Arizona


74 Ariz. 269; 247 P.2d 617; 1952 Ariz. LEXIS 200; 33 A.L.R.2d 1102



July 16, 1952 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizens appealed from the judgment of an Arizona trial court, which dismissed their complaint for an injunction to prohibit defendant secretary of state from certifying to the clerks of the several boards of supervisors, for inclusion on the official ballot to be submitted to the voters at the general election, a referendum proposed by the legislature.


OVERVIEW: By resolution, the state legislature enacted and ordered the submission to the people of a measure repealing an initiative measure. The statute subject to the proposed repeal had been initiated by petition and adopted by a majority of the votes cast thereon, but not by a majority vote of the qualified electors in the state. The court held that the legislature was not precluded from the proposed repeal by Ariz. Const. art. 4, pt. 1, § 1(6), which provided that the veto power of the governor, or the power of the legislature, to repeal or amend did not extend to initiative or referendum measures approved by a majority vote of the qualified electors. The court held that the phrase "a majority vote of the qualified electors" was to be literally interpreted and did not mean a majority of the votes cast upon the particular measure. The court held that the reference of a measure by the legislature to the people was a part of the exercise of the legislative function and was not subject to injunction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment dismissing the citizens' complaint for an injunction.

 

618 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Harold


No. 1032


Supreme Court of Arizona


74 Ariz. 210; 246 P.2d 178; 1952 Ariz. LEXIS 190



July 16, 1952 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought certification for review after moving in a trial court (Arizona) to quash an information charging violations of Ariz. Code Ann. §§ 66-156 and 66-157 (1939). He claimed that §§ 66-156 and 66-157 were unconstitutional as violative of Ariz. Const. art. IV, pt. 2, § 13; art. II, § 10; and art. II, § 15; and that the laws also represented an unwarranted, arbitrary, capricious, unlawful, and unconstitutional exercise of police powers.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who had been previously convicted of the offense of driving while intoxicated (DWI) sought quashor of a two-count information on the grounds that the statutes were unconstitutional and that the legislature exceeded its authority in the passage thereof. The court rejected all of the claims and held that the statutes were constitutional. After determining that there was no merit to defendant's claim, pursuant to Ariz. Const. art. IV, pt. 2, § 13, that their subjects were not included in the title of the act of which they were a part, the court declared that the legislature had ample police power to pass legislation imposing penalties for DWI offenses. That power extended to public and private property and was not limited to public highways. Moreover, neither the inclusion therein of standards relating to alcohol content in a driver's blood nor the penalties imposed for violations thereof offended constitutional guarantees. Finally, the court ruled that it was proper for a trial court to consider whether a defendant had a prior DWI conviction in imposing sentence for a subsequent offense and to impose a more serious penalty in connection with a subsequent conviction.


OUTCOME: The court held that the challenged laws were constitutional in all respects.

 

619 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Bennett


No. 1014


Supreme Court of Arizona


74 Ariz. 6; 242 P.2d 840; 1952 Ariz. LEXIS 156



April 7, 1952 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona), which quashed count II of an information filed against defendant alleging violation of Ariz. Code Ann. § 50-1304 (1939), which regulated the preparation and processing of horsemeat products. The trial court had rendered a judgment of not guilty on the first count alleging violation of Ariz. Code Ann. § 50-1306 (1939).


OVERVIEW: The trial court found defendant not guilty on the first count of the indictment, but found him guilty on the second count. The trial court then granted defendant's motion for a new trial and his motion to quash the second count of the indictment. Defendant alleged that § 50-1304 was unconstitutional. The court reversed and held that the act was constitutional. The court found that the act clearly denounced the offense charged and the language employed by the prosecuting attorney adequately conformed with all the provisions of state statutes prescribing the requisite contents of indictments and informations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded for further proceedings.

 

620 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Phoenix v. Pensinger


No. 5405


Supreme Court of Arizona


73 Ariz. 420; 242 P.2d 546; 1952 Ariz. LEXIS 268



March 31, 1952 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The City of Phoenix challenged a judgment of the trial court (Arizona), which issued a judgment in an action filed by appellee, the Phoenix City Magistrate, declaring that an amendment to the Phoenix City Charter was unconstitutional and invalid in its entirety. The amendment provided the qualifications for the City Magistrate office, the manner of selection, appointment, and removal, and a definite term of office and minimum salary limitations.


OVERVIEW: Ariz. Const. art. VI, § 11 provided that judges were ineligible for any office other than a judicial one during the term for which they were elected. Because the amendment attempted to use judges as a nominating board for candidates for the City Magistrate position, it clearly violated the Constitution. However, the City argued that the entire amendment should not have been invalidated because the remaining parts passed muster with the unconstitutional part stricken out. On appeal, the court disagreed. When the unconstitutional part of legislation was stricken out, that which remained had to be sustained if the remainder was complete in itself and was capable of being executed in accordance with the apparent legislative intent. In the case at bar, it was the clear intent of the amendment to require the City Magistrate appointee to be a licensed and practicing attorney. That requirement was inextricably bound up in the invalid method of providing for selection; without reference to the invalid provision, there was no guide as to what qualifications the officer had to possess. Thus, the remaining parts could not stand alone and, as such, the entire amendment was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

621 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Church v. Baker


No. 5441


Supreme Court of Arizona


73 Ariz. 350; 241 P.2d 790; 1952 Ariz. LEXIS 255



March 17, 1952 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Employment Security Commission sought review of the judgment of the trial court (Arizona), which determined that it had no valid lien in plaintiff assignees' foreclosure action.


OVERVIEW: The mortgagor, who was an employer under the terms of the Employment Security Act of Arizona, obtained mortgages for the purchase of real property. On June 14 and 16, 1949, the mortgagee recorded the mortgages. On December 2, 1949, the Commission filed a lien claim for contributions due from the mortgagor in March, June, and September 1949. On April 1, 1950, the assignees obtained the mortgages from the mortgagee. Subsequently, the assignees filed an action to foreclose the mortgages. The trial court found that the Commission had no lien on the property. The Commission appealed. The court affirmed the judgment because Ariz. Code Ann. § 56-1014h was unconstitutional and void as against prior recorded mortgages taken in good faith and without actual or constructive notice of the Commission's claimed lien.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the determination that the Commission lacked a valid lien.

 

622 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Myerson v. Sakrison


No. 5365


Supreme Court of Arizona


73 Ariz. 308; 240 P.2d 1198; 1952 Ariz. LEXIS 247



February 26, 1952 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied March 18, 1952.



DISPOSITION: Modified and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant assignees of a mortgage challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Pima County (Arizona) for appellee Employment Security Commission in the assignees' action to secure judgment on a note and foreclose the mortgage. The trial court held that by the provisions of Ariz. Code Ann. § 56-1014h (1939) (Act), the Commission's lien was prior and superior to the mortgage.


OVERVIEW: An employer was in default to the Commission for contributions. He delivered a promissory note and a realty mortgage to the assignor, which was recorded 16 days before the Commission recorded its lien. The assignor had no actual knowledge, or notice from any source whatsoever, of a lien of any kind. The court said a mortgagee had been protected by being able to depend upon the public records as to priority of liens and mortgages. If such protection was taken away, which was the effect of the lien authorized by the Act, then such result was unreasonable and destructive of inherent rights and the commercial life of the state would be impaired. Under the Act as written, the lien authorized might have had the effect of taking the property of a third person to pay the tax owed by an employer without a hearing or due process of law, and would offend, not only U.S. Const. amend. XIV, but also Ariz. Const. art. 2, § 4.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was modified by striking that part giving priority to the Employment Security Commission lien over the mortgage lien of the assignees. The case was remanded to the trial court for the entry of judgment in accordance with the views expressed.

 

623 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Pressley v. Industrial Comm'n


No. 5431


Supreme Court of Arizona


73 Ariz. 22; 236 P.2d 1011; 1951 Ariz. LEXIS 147



October 29, 1951 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Award set aside in part and affirmed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner injured worker sought a rehearing from the court in his challenge to a decision from respondent Industrial Accident Board (Arizona), which denied the injured worker the right to sue a third party for injuries that the injured worker received for which he was granted accident benefits.


OVERVIEW: The injured worker suffered an industrial accident for which he sought compensation under the Ariz. Workmen's Compensation Act. The injured worker also intended to bring an action against a third party for the accident. The Industrial Accident Board determined that it was primarily liable for both accident benefits and compensation, and it ordered that the injured worker not bring an action against the third party. The injured worker asserted that by electing to pursue his action against the third party, the workmen's compensation fund was primarily liable only for accident benefits but secondarily liable for compensation benefits under Ariz. Code Ann. § 56-949, and then only to the extent he failed to recover from the third party as much as he was entitled. The court set aside as void the order that denied the injured worker the right to proceed against the third party, but affirmed the award of accident benefits, because it determined that the Industrial Accident Board did not have the power to decide questions of law that are unnecessary to determine the matters before it or to be entering what was in effect a judgment declaring the rights of parties to make elections.


OUTCOME: The court set aside as void the decision by the Industrial Accident Board that denied the injured worker the right to sue a third party but affirmed the decision that granted the injured worker accident benefits.

 

624 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Gee


No. 1001


Supreme Court of Arizona


73 Ariz. 47; 236 P.2d 1029; 1951 Ariz. LEXIS 151



October 29, 1951 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The judge of the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona) asked, by order of certification, for answers to four questions involving the validity of the Arizona Basic Science Act, Ariz. Code Ann. art. 2, ch. 67 (1939). The questions arose in the action charging defendant with practicing chiropractic without a license and practicing without a valid certificate in basic sciences.


OVERVIEW: During defendant's prosecution for practicing chiropractic without valid certification and licensing, the superior court referred four certified questions to the court. The court first held that Ariz. Code Ann. art. 2, ch. 67 was not unconstitutional as an unlawful delegation of legislative power to the Arizona State Board of Examiners in the Basic Sciences. The Act did not delegate the responsibility of defining the basic sciences and delegated administrative, rather than legislative authority to the Board. The court also stated that the Act was not unconstitutional as violating the federal and State Due Process Clauses. Several provisions insured that due process was guaranteed to persons covered under the Act. The court further held that the Act did not violate Ariz. Const. art. 4, pt. 2, § 19(13). The Act was not special legislation, but general, in that it embraced the whose State and operated upon all persons therein who practiced the medical arts. Finally, the court concluded that the Act was not unconstitutionally vague because the Arizona legislature sufficiently and adequately defined the terms used in the Act.


OUTCOME: The court answered the certified questions in the negative.

 

625 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


In re Twenty-One Slot Machs.


No. 5314


Supreme Court of Arizona


72 Ariz. 408; 236 P.2d 733; 1951 Ariz. LEXIS 249



October 15, 1951 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant private club owners sought review from the judgments and orders of the Superior Court of Pima County (Arizona), which decreed certain slot machines illegal per se, directed the destruction thereof, and denied the owners' motion to return money contained therein at the time of seizure.


OVERVIEW: A county sheriff seized, without a search warrant or other legal process, certain slot machines and money contents on the premises of the private clubs. The clubs' managers were ultimately acquitted of criminal charges, but the machines were adjudged illegal per se and destroyed under the authority of 1947 Ariz. Sess. Laws 110, § 7. From the owners' appeal, the court reversed and remanded. The court held that the trial court erred in ordering the slot machines destroyed because § 7 was unconstitutional in that the subject matter was not expressed directly or by implication in the title of the Act, as required by Ariz. Const. art. 4, pt. 2, § 13. The title did not suggest that property being labeled contraband was subject to destruction and was therefore void. The trial court also erred in denying the owners' request to return the money that had been inside the slot machines because the money had never been used as evidence, was not being held as evidence, and had never been in custodia legis because the proceeding had been extra judicium. As such, the court reversed and ordered that the money be returned to the owners.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments and orders of the trial court, which had decreed certain slot machines property illegal per se, directed the destruction thereof, and denied the owners' motion to return money contained therein at the time of seizure. The court appealed remanded with instructions that the trial court grant the owners' motion for summary judgment and their motion for return of the money.

 

626 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Pressley v. Industrial Comm'n


No. 5431


Supreme Court of Arizona


72 Ariz. 299; 233 P.2d 1082; 1951 Ariz. LEXIS 230



July 13, 1951 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Granted October 9, 1951. See 236 P.2d 1011.



DISPOSITION: Award set aside.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee sought review of an award of respondent Industrial Commission of Arizona, which retained jurisdiction of his claim for workmen's compensation and in effect denied him the right to sue a third party.


OVERVIEW: The employee was injured by a gas explosion. A workman's claim for compensation was filed and the Commission found that the employee suffered an injury by an accident arising out of and in the course of his employment. The employee returned the compensation check he received and executed an election of remedies to proceed under Ariz. Code Ann. § 56-949 (1939) against a third party. The court found that the legislature intended to treat compensation and accident benefits as two separate and distinct entities, and that all injured employees should receive accident benefits, including those who elected to pursue a third party. The court ruled that in order for the employee's application for an accident benefits award to have been a binding election, he must have had knowledge of his alternative remedy to pursue the third party. The court determined that the Commission did not base its award on whether the employee had the requisite knowledge, but rather, that his application precluded him from pursuing his remedy against a third party. Setting aside the award, the court held that the Commission's decision was not justified by the state workmen's compensation act.


OUTCOME: The court set the award aside.

Edwards v. State Bd. of Barber Examiners


No. 5356


Supreme Court of Arizona


72 Ariz. 108; 231 P.2d 450; 1951 Ariz. LEXIS 196



May 10, 1951 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff barber appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona) dismissing his complaint, wherein he sought a declaratory judgment to adjudge that the price-fixing provisions of the Barber Act, Ariz. Code Ann. art. 1, ch. 67 (1939) (Act) were unconstitutional. The barber also asked for an injunction restraining the enforcement of price-fixing provisions by defendant State Board of Barber Examiners.


OVERVIEW: The barber's shop was near the Mexican border and haircuts were available there cheaper than the price set by the Board, thus depriving him of business. The barber contended that the fixing of minimum prices for barbering services had no reasonable or substantial relation to the public health, safety, or the general welfare, and hence, the Act that provided for price-fixing violated Ariz. Const. art. 2, § 4 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court said the police power was not unrestrained. The Board failed to indicate, or to establish any logical relationship whatsoever, the basis for its assumption that the Act was not arbitrary and wholly unwarranted; nor did it point out any possible facts which, if assumed to be true, would place them within the purview of the operation of the rule; nor was the court able to discover any from the reported cases or otherwise conceive of any. The court held that the Act was therefore unconstitutional and void as a violation of Ariz. Const. art. 2, § 4.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the cause remanded, with directions that the order of dismissal was to be vacated and the motion to dismiss overruled, and further proceedings were not to be in conflict with the opinion.

 

628 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Lockwood v. Jordan


No. 5508


Supreme Court of Arizona


72 Ariz. 77; 231 P.2d 428; 1951 Ariz. LEXIS 191



May 7, 1951 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Alternative writ of mandamus made permanent.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Post Auditor of the State of Arizona brought an original proceeding against respondent State Auditor of the State of Arizona to compel her to issue to him a warrant in payment of his salary as post auditor. The state auditor contended that the act creating the post auditor position was unconstitutional as violating the separation of powers doctrine and that the failure of the legislature to confirm his appointment created a vacancy.


OVERVIEW: H.B. 74, ch. 28 created the office of post auditor. The act provided that the post auditor would be appointed by the president of the senate and the speaker of the house of representatives with the advice and consent of a majority of the senate and of the house of representatives. The act further provided that the first post auditor be appointed for a three-year period. The court found that there was no violation of the doctrine of separation of powers pursuant to Ariz. Const. art. 3 because, where the constitution provided that the duties of the state auditor were to be "prescribed by law", there were no express restrictions imposed upon the legislature with respect to the powers and duties of the state auditor. The court held, therefore, that the legislature was acting within the scope of its legislative authority in providing for appointment of a post auditor. Finally, the court concluded that the failure of the legislature to confirm the appointment of the post auditor did not create a vacancy because, having been lawfully appointed, he continued in office until the end of his terms unless his appointment was affirmatively disapproved by the legislature.


OUTCOME: The court made the alternative writ of mandamus permanent.

 

629 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Findlay v. Board of Supervisors


No. 5328


Supreme Court of Arizona


72 Ariz. 58; 230 P.2d 526; 1951 Ariz. LEXIS 188; 24 A.L.R.2d 841



April 23, 1951 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment reversed with instructions to enter judgment reinstituting desist order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner physicians appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Mohave County (Arizona), which quashed its earlier writ of certiorari against respondent board of supervisors (the board), dissolved the stay that had been ordered at the time the writ was issued, and dismissed the petition. The physicians challenged a regulation that denied their county hospital privileges for failure to assist other physicians.


OVERVIEW: Mohave County, Ariz., Regulation 111 required any doctor with privileges at the county hospital to assist other doctors upon request. The regulation did not provide for notice or hearing before revocation of staff privileges. The board revoked the privileges of the physicians without detailed reasons. On appeal, the court held that a petition for a writ of certiorari was a proper mechanism for a challenge to the jurisdiction of the board in its enforcement of a regulation that the physicians alleged to be so unreasonable and so unjust as to be unconstitutional. The court found that while localities could regulate and control their hospitals and prescribe reasonable rules and regulations to be followed by doctors using the facilities, the board could not arbitrarily preclude a regularly licensed physician from the right to practice in a county hospital, so long as such physician stayed within the law. The court held that the regulation invaded the personal liberty and contractual rights of both the patient and the physician and that enforcement of the rule would enslave a physician. The court also held that the lack of notice and a hearing offended due process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that had quashed the writ and dissolved the stay. The court instructed the trial court to reinstitute the desist order.

 

630 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Dennis v. Jordan


No. 5408


Supreme Court of Arizona


71 Ariz. 430; 229 P.2d 692; 1951 Ariz. LEXIS 281



April 2, 1951 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied April 24, 1951.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sought review of an order of the trial court (Arizona), which sustained the constitutionality of the Public Employees' Retirement Act (Act), Ariz. Code Ann. §§ 12-801 to 12-8281939, and held that the employee had no legal standing to maintain the action.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the employee argued that the Act was unconstitutional and void because the it was not initiated, submitted, or referred to a vote of the people or enacted in conformity with Ariz. Const. art. 4, pt. 1, § 1. However, the court held that once a law was passed and duly promulgated, it was conclusively presumed to have been regularly enacted and was invulnerable to attack upon the ground of a defective title appearing on the ballot. The employee further argued that the Act was unconstitutional because it unlawfully delegated legislative power to the board of trustees and the actuary, where the Act allowed them to fashion tabular standards as they deemed fit. However, the court held that the Act did not unlawfully delegate legislative power but merely authorized the board of trustees, with the aid of their actuary, to find the necessary actuarial facts and prescribe the executive details for carrying out the legislation. Therefore, the court held that the Act was constitutional as applied to state employees, but determined that the employee could not challenge the constitutionality of the Act as applied to municipalities.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court was affirmed insofar as it applied to employees of the state; the court reserved passing upon the validity of the Act insofar as it applied to political subdivisions of the state.

Arizona Corp. Comm'n v. Mountain States Tel. & Tel. Co.


No. 5352


Supreme Court of Arizona


71 Ariz. 404; 228 P.2d 749; 1951 Ariz. LEXIS 276



March 12, 1951 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the Arizona Corporation Commission and others, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona) which set aside the orders of the commission which denied the application of appellee telephone company to raise its telephone rates for its intrastate and long distance telephone service previously fixed by the commission on the ground that the existing rates were not just and reasonable and were confiscatory.


OVERVIEW: The telephone company appealed the commission's denial of its application for a rate increase. The trial court set aside the commission's orders and granted the telephone company's motion for temporary relief by allowing it to charge certain increased rates. The trial court found that the commission's order was unlawful and remanded the case to the commission for a determination of a fair rate of return to be allowed and for the fixing of just and reasonable rates. Pending the final determination, the trial court allowed the continuation of the temporary rates subject to the telephone company posting a bond to secure a refund of the temporary rates if found to be in excess of the rates finally established by the commission. The commission appealed, contending that the trial court had no inherent power to provide relief pending the correction of the unlawful commission orders. The court held that, although the trial court could not make utility rates, it could, in the exercise of its jurisdiction, attach to the judgment enjoining the confiscatory rates a condition that the telephone company could not collect rates in excess of a stated amount to avoid confiscation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

United States Fidelity & Guar. Co. v. Frohmiller


No. 5233


Supreme Court of Arizona


71 Ariz. 377; 227 P.2d 1007; 1951 Ariz. LEXIS 271



February 26, 1951 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment reversed with direction to dismiss complaint.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of a judgment of the state trial court (Arizona) holding plaintiffs, who prevailed in prior suit brought by defendant taxpayer for recovery of state monies, were entitled to attorneys fees from bond posted by taxpayer.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were state officers and sureties who filed an action for damages and attorney fees following a suit in which a taxpayer alleged plaintiffs illegally paid out state monies. As part of that action, the taxpayer posted a bond. Plaintiffs sought to recover its fees and damages from the bond. Defendant surety company moved to dismiss and the trial court denied the motion. 633 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State Tax Comm'n v. Quebedeaux Chevrolet


No. 5367


Supreme Court of Arizona


71 Ariz. 280; 226 P.2d 549; 1951 Ariz. LEXIS 255



January 22, 1951 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant State Tax Commission of Arizona (commission) appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona) in favor of plaintiff taxpayer in the taxpayer's action to recover excise taxes paid under the Excise Revenue Act of 1935, Ariz. Code Ann. §§ 73-1301 to 73-1334 (1939) (Act).


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer filed a complaint seeking to recover excise taxes paid for the sale of tangible personal property. The taxpayer sold such items under a privilege license issued pursuant to the Act. After a trial, the court entered judgment in favor of the taxpayer. The commission appealed. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court. The court found that the tax under the Act was not a true sales tax but a tax upon the privilege of engaging in business within the state. The court held that the two percent tax imposed upon the taxpayer by Ariz. Code Ann. § 73-1303(d)(1) constituted a part of the taxpayer's gross proceeds of sales or gross income from business. The tax pursuant to § 73-1303(d)(1) was part of the selling price which formed the base upon which the amount of the tax was levied. Contrary to the taxpayer's argument, § 73-1331(c) did not show the legislative intent was not to include the tax as part of the gross income because it would have been stated in clear and unmistakable language. In addition, including the two percent tax in the gross income was the interpretation given by the commission which was charged with enforcing the Act.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court in favor of the taxpayer in the taxpayer's action against the commission to recover excise taxes paid under protest.

 

634 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Coursey


No. 1003


Supreme Court of Arizona


71 Ariz. 227; 225 P.2d 713; 1950 Ariz. LEXIS 176



December 11, 1950 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the trial court (Arizona), which convicted him of robbery and kidnapping with intent to rob. Defendant was sentenced to not less than 20 nor more than 25 years on each count, the sentences to run concurrently.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of robbery and kidnapping with intent to rob. He was sentenced to not less than 20 nor more than 25 years on each count, the sentences to run concurrently. On appeal, the court affirmed because (1) there was no legal basis for defendant to file his "plea in bar" and later a "plea to the merits and in bar" because he was limited to a motion to quash; (2) the only ground specified in the motion to quash was not one of the grounds enumerated under Ariz. Code Ann. § 44-1005, and, thus, could not legally form the basis for such a motion; (3) the motion to quash not having been timely made was waived; (4) the crime of robbery was sustained by the evidence even though actual ownership of the property was in one other than the complainant because one who had the right of possession as against the thief was, so far as the latter was concerned, the owner; and (5) Ariz. Code Ann. § 43-3203 was not unconstitutional because the parole board never had the power to grant a parole to a prisoner until his minimum sentence had been served, and, thus, any power that the board previously had was not affected by § 43-3203.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which convicted defendant of robbery and kidnapping with intent to rob and sentenced defendant to not less than 20 nor more than 25 years on each count, the sentences to run concurrently.

 

635 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Frye v. South Phoenix Volunteer Fire Co.


No. 5332


Supreme Court of Arizona


71 Ariz. 163; 224 P.2d 651; 1950 Ariz. LEXIS 165



November 13, 1950 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county supervisors sought review of a judgment of the trial court (Arizona) granting appellee volunteer fire department a writ of mandamus in its action to compel payment of a sum certain for required equipment and maintenance. The supervisors claimed that language in Ariz. Code Ann. § 16-2007 (1939) was permissive only and gave them the discretion to decide whether or not to make the disputed payments.


OVERVIEW: Ariz. Code Ann. § 16-2007 directed county supervisors to estimate the expenses of volunteer fire departments, to levy taxes, and to distribute funds for fire equipment and maintenance. When the supervisors failed to do so, the volunteer fire department sought a writ of mandamus to compel the payment. The trial court granted the writ, and the court affirmed its judgment. The court rejected the supervisors' contention that the word "may" in § 16-2007 was permissive or that it gave them discretion to decide not to make the award. The statute's purpose was to provide fire protection for certain communities outside the protection of a municipal fire department, and those privileges and immunities were provided at public expense. The legislature did not intent to vest discretion in the supervisors because there were no standards in the statute by which their discretion could have been measured. A delegation of absolute and arbitrary power was unconstitutional. Because the statute lacked any standards, limitations, or policies to guide the supervisors, it was apparent that the legislature did not give them the claimed discretion and they were required to make the payment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court granting the volunteer fire department a writ of mandamus compelling the supervisors to pay the statutory amounts required for equipment and maintenance.

 

636 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Roberts v. Spray


No. 5325


Supreme Court of Arizona


71 Ariz. 60; 223 P.2d 808; 1950 Ariz. LEXIS 150



November 6, 1950 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of the order of the trial court (Arizona), which dismissed their declaratory judgment action under Ariz. Code Ann. § 27-701 et seq. against appellees, members of the county board of supervisors and attorney general, seeking to have the Hospital District Act of 1949 declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The Hospital District Act (Act) created a mechanism for the creation of hospital districts. Among other provisions, the Act outlined the requirements to be met before an individual was able to vote in the district. The Act stated that any resident real property owner in the district was able to vote in an election. The taxpayers filed its declaratory judgment action, arguing that the Act violated the equal protection clause of the United States Constitution because the Act was ambiguous and indefinite as to who qualified as a voter and that the Act violated Ariz. Const. art. 7, §§ 2, 13 because the Act was in conflict with the definition of qualified voters therein. The trial court dismissed the action. On appeal, the court affirmed the order of the trial court. The Act did not violate the equal protection clause because the definition of voter in the Act was clear and unambiguous. Further, Ariz. Const. art. 7, §§ 2, 13 were self-executing constitutional provisions. Therefore, any statutes relating to their subject matter only needed to be in harmony with them. The Act was in harmony with these constitutional provisions because it merely added to the requirements therein.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court, which dismissed the taxpayers' declaratory judgment action against the members of the county board of supervisors and the attorney general.

 

637 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Haggard v. Industrial Comm'n


No. 5263


Supreme Court of Arizona


71 Ariz. 91; 223 P.2d 915; 1950 Ariz. LEXIS 156



November 3, 1950 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment reversed and cause remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employer challenged an order of the superior court of Maricopa County (Arizona), which found that certain individuals in and around the employer's place of business were "employees" within the meaning of the Workers' Compensation Act, Ariz. Code Ann. § 56-901 et seq., and were insurable at the employer's expense.


OVERVIEW: Appellee Industrial Commission issued an order finding that certain temporary workers in and around the employer's dog and horse racing operation were employees within the meaning of the Workers' Compensation Act (Act). The order had the effect of requiring the employer to insure said employees. The employer instituted a class action on behalf of himself and others similarly situated. The trial court entered an adverse judgment and the employer appealed claiming multiple assignments of error. On review, the court rejected the employer's argument that the trial court erred when it refused to render judgment under the Declaratory Judgment Act. The court reasoned that the Act established a procedure of its own and that the procedure furnished a remedy which was substantial, complete, and exclusive in compensation cases. However, the court did reverse the trial court's finding that employer was responsible to insure certain categories of employees. The court reasoned that the employees were independent contractors and not subject to the provisions of the Act. Finally, the court remanded the case to determine whether a category of photographers were independent contractors or not.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that found that the individuals in question were employees with the meaning of the Worker's Compensation Act and insurable at the employer's expense. The court remanded the case with instructions to grant a new trial in order to determine the status of the class of persons designated as photographers.

 

638 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Peters v. Frye


No. 5251


Supreme Court of Arizona


71 Ariz. 30; 223 P.2d 176; 1950 Ariz. LEXIS 144



October 23, 1950, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, owners of land within a proposed irrigation water delivery district, challenged the order of the Superior Court (Arizona) entered in favor of appellees, the Board of Supervisors of Maricopa County, dismissing the owners' application for a writ of mandamus and quashing an alternative writ previously issued.


OVERVIEW: The owners petitioned the Board of Supervisors to organize an irrigation water district. The petition was examined and found by the Board to conform to Ariz. Code Ann. § 75-500 et seq. (1939). A bond was fixed and was provided by the owners. A hearing was set to afford the owners within the proposed district an opportunity to appear and object to the organization. The Board heard the objections and refused to organize the district. The owners then filed the action for writ of mandamus compelling the Board to proceed under statute to organize the district. The trial court dismissed the application and quashed the alternative writ previously issued and rendered a judgment for the Board. The board contended that the statute gave it broad discretionary power to refuse to organize. On appeal, the court held that the statute authorizing the Board to hear objections to proposals to create the district conferred discretionary power upon the Board to refuse to organize even though all statutory procedural requirements for formation of the district had been met. Further, the Board did not abuse its discretion in refusing to organize, as there was no present need for such district.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

639 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Thrift Hardware & Supply Co. v. Phoenix


No. 5211


Supreme Court of Arizona


71 Ariz. 21; 222 P.2d 994; 1950 Ariz. LEXIS 142; 22 A.L.R.2d 810



October 17, 1950 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded with direction.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff seller appealed a decision from the trial court (Arizona), which entered judgment for defendant city in the seller's action to enjoin portions of the city's plumbing ordinance that prevented the sale of certain plumbing fixtures.


OVERVIEW: The seller was a dealer of plumbing fixtures. It sold supplies it manufactured itself, as well as other fixtures that it did not make. These fixtures were either new and perfect or new, but imperfect, and sold at a discount. The seller brought an action to enjoin enforcement of various city ordinances regulating plumbing fixtures. On appeal from a decision for the city in the seller's action, the court held that: (1) where an ordinance prohibiting the sale of "broken or defective" fixtures could be used to prohibit the sale of imperfect fixtures that still functioned perfectly, the city could not use the ordinance to prevent the sale or use of such fixtures; (2) the city could not engage in discriminatory enforcement of an ordinance regulating the labeling of plumbing fixtures when the evidence showed that the ordinance was enforced against the seller, but not against other stores; (3) where the evidence showed that concrete water tanks made by the seller did not endanger public health, an ordinance prohibiting the installation of such tanks was void; and (4) where the trial court found part of an ordinance unconstitutional, it should have enjoined its enforcement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court and remanded the case for further proceedings.

 

640 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Moore v. Bolin


No. 5358


Supreme Court of Arizona


70 Ariz. 354; 220 P.2d 850; 1950 Ariz. LEXIS 238



July 15, 1950 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state officer sought review of a decision from the trial court (Arizona), which granted appellee secretary of state's motion for summary judgment in the state officer's declaratory judgment action. The state officer had sought a declaration that a statute that prohibited an incumbent state officer from running for another state office was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The state officer brought a declaratory judgment action against the secretary of state. The state officer sought to have declared unconstitutional a statute that prohibited an incumbent state officer in mid-term from being a candidate for another state office. The trial court granted the secretary of state's motion for summary judgment. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that: (1) the facts pleaded by the state officer did not show a present existing controversy that permitted the court to adjudicate any present rights; and (2) the state officer's claims merely showed an intent to do certain things in the future, all of which were dependent on future events and contingencies that were within the control of the state officer.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which entered a summary judgment in favor of the secretary of state in the state official's declaratory judgment action.

 

641 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


In re Lewkowitz


No. 5235


Supreme Court of Arizona


70 Ariz. 325; 220 P.2d 229; 1950 Ariz. LEXIS 234



June 29, 1950 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Order heretofore entered granting respondents' motions to quash and dismiss vacated and ordered that motions to vacate, quash and dismiss in order to show cause in the matter be denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner State Bar of Arizona (state bar) filed a motion for rehearing in its disciplinary action against respondent attorneys.


OVERVIEW: The state bar commenced disciplinary proceedings against the attorneys. The attorneys filed motions to quash, vacate, and dismiss, claiming that the title to the State Bar Act, Ariz. Code Ann. ch. 32, art. 3 (1939) violated Ariz. Const. art. 4, pt. 2, § 3. The court granted the motions and the state bar filed a motion for rehearing. The court granted the motion for rehearing, vacated its earlier decision, and denied the attorneys' motions. The court determined that the title was subject to only one reasonable construction. The court stated that the title to the State Bar Act, Ariz. Code Ann. ch. 23, art. 3 (1939) was sufficient to meet the constitutional requirement because the only reasonable conclusion that could be drawn from the title by the average legislator and those having an interest in or affected by the legislation proposing integration was that contained in such legislation there would be found such closely allied, relevant, and germane provisions as those having to do with disciplinary action. The court further held that the attorneys were not denied due process either on account of the shortness of the notice or the refusal to grant a continuance.


OUTCOME: The court vacated its earlier decision that granted the attorneys' motions to quash and dismiss, and denied the motions, in the state bar's disciplinary action against the attorneys.

 

642 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Double Seven Corp.


No. 1000


Supreme Court of Arizona


70 Ariz. 287; 219 P.2d 776; 1950 Ariz. LEXIS 226; 19 A.L.R.2d 1007



June 19, 1950 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: By direct information, plaintiff County Attorney for Maricopa County accused defendant corporation of unlawfully offering to sell horse meat for human consumption that was unlawfully labeled and sold under the name of hamburger, in violation of the provisions of Ariz. Code Ann. art. 13, ch. 50 (1939). The Superior Court for the County of Maricopa (Arizona) denied the corporation's motion to quash. The trial court certified the issue to the court.


OVERVIEW: The corporation questioned the constitutionality of Ariz. Code Ann. art. 13, ch. 50 (1939), entitled Horsemeat for Human Consumption, or the horse meat act. The corporation argued that the Fourteenth Amendment demanded that statutes passed under the police power have some natural and reasonable basis and relationship to the object to be accomplished. The court held that the State had the right to regulate the livestock industry, particularly that portion devoted to the slaughter and sale of animals for human consumption. Although the horsemeat act required horsemeat to be slaughtered in an establishment separate and apart from other livestock, the court held that this was not a denial of the Equal Protection Clause because the legislation sought to protect the public interest from fraud. By requiring horsemeat to be slaughtered in separate establishments, the legislature sought to make it more difficult for the unscrupulous to sell horsemeat products in place of other meat products, or the possibility of the occurrence of innocent mistakes by confusing horsemeat with other meats. The court held that prevention of fraud was a valid exercise of the State's police power.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the action to the trial court for disposition of the case on its merits.
